The Collection of the Quran: Sunni Perspective
Nasafi presents the Sunni position on the collection of the Quran and the ruling on denying it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on how the Quran was collected and the ruling on denying any part of the Uthmanic mushaf.

Core Idea
The Quran was compiled by Abu Bakr and publicized by Uthman with unanimous agreement of the Companions.

This passage from Imam Nasafi addresses a disputed historical issue: who compiled the Quran? The Rafidah claim it was Ali, while Ahl al-Sunnah affirm it was Uthman who made public the compilation started by Abu Bakr. Nasafi also explains why denial of any verse from the Uthmanic mushaf amounts to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a narrative style to present the historical sequence.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage begins with the Rafidah claim and then gives the Sunni counterclaim. Identify who is attributed with compiling the Quran according to each group.
The Sunni claim is that Uthman publicized the compilation, but the initial gathering was by Abu Bakr.
3
Trace the historical timeline
Follow the events from the Prophet's death through Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman. Note why each caliph did or did not publicize the Quran.
Abu Bakr gathered it but was occupied with war; Umar was occupied with conquests; Uthman saw disagreement and brought it forth.
4
Understand the ruling
Nasafi concludes with a strong ruling: denying any verse from Uthman's mushaf is disbelief because of the consensus of the Companions. Reflect on why this ruling is based on ijma.
This shows the high status of the Uthmanic codex in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الرافضة: الإمامُ القرءان الذي جمعه القرآن علي رضي الله عنه. وقال أهل السنة والجماعة الإمام الذي جمعه عثمان بن عفان رضي الله عنه؛ لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لما توفي جمع أبو بكر رضي الله عنه القرآن، وكان يقرؤه فلم يتفرغ لإظهاره، لأنه كان مشغولاً بقتال أهل اليمامة، وكان أبو بكر رضي الله عنه في الخلافة سنتين، فلما توفي لم يظهره عمر رضِيَ الله عنه، لأنه كان مشغولاً بفتح خراسان وغيره. فلما كان في زمان عثمان رضي الله عنه اختلفوا في القرآن. قال عثمان رضي الله عنه: إنكم اختلفتم فَمَنْ بعدكم أشد اختلافا، فجلس عثمان رضِيَ الله عنه، وأخرج الذي جمعه أبو بكر رضي الله عنه، فأظهره على الصحابة إلا أنه ينسب إلى عثمان رضي الله عنه، لأنه هو الذي أظهره، واتفقت الصحابة رضيَ الله عنهم على ذلك. فكل من أنكر آيةٌ من مصحف عثمان رضي الله عنه فإنه يكفر، لأن مصحف عثمان رضِيَ الله عنه هو الذي أجمعت عليه الصحابة رضي الله عنهم [أجميعن].
The Rafidah said: The imam of the Quran which the Quran gathered is Ali, may Allah be pleased with him. And Ahl al-Sunnah wal-Jama'ah said: The imam who gathered it is Uthman ibn Affan, may Allah be pleased with him; because when the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, died, Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, gathered the Quran, and he used to recite it but he did not have the leisure to bring it forth, because he was occupied with fighting the people of al-Yamamah, and Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, was in the caliphate for two years. When he died, Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, did not bring it forth, because he was occupied with the conquest of Khurasan and other places. Then in the time of Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, they differed concerning the Quran. Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'You have differed, and those after you will differ more severely.' So Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, sat down and brought out what Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, had gathered, and he made it known to the Companions, except that it is attributed to Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, because he was the one who made it known, and the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them all, agreed upon that. So whoever denies a verse from the mushaf of Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, then he disbelieves, because the mushaf of Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, is that which the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, unanimously agreed upon [all of them].
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a historical and doctrinal argument defending the Sunni position on the collection of the Quran. It begins by stating the Rafidah claim that Imam Ali compiled the Quran, and then gives the Sunni counterclaim that it was Uthman. The author provides a sequential narrative: after the Prophet's death, Abu Bakr gathered the Quran but did not publicize it due to the Yamama war. Umar also did not publicize it due to conquests. During Uthman's caliphate, disagreements arose about the Quran. Uthman warned that later generations would have even greater disagreement. So he brought out Abu Bakr's compilation and presented it publicly to the Companions. Although Uthman is credited, the author clarifies that the actual compilation was Abu Bakr's work; Uthman merely made it known. The author then concludes with a strong ruling: denial of any verse from Uthman's mushaf constitutes disbelief, because the Companions unanimously agreed upon it. The phrase 'أجميعن' (all of them) emphasizes complete consensus.

Key Terms
جمع القرآن
مصحف عثمان
إجماع الصحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Uthman compiled the Quran from scratch without any prior compilation.
Correction: The text shows that Abu Bakr had already gathered the Quran, and Uthman only publicized that existing compilation.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunnah, who compiled the Quran that was later made public by Uthman?
A) Ali ibn Abi Talib
B) Abu Bakr al-Siddiq
C) Uthman ibn Affan
D) Umar ibn al-Khattab

Key Takeaway
The Sunni belief is that the Quran was compiled by Abu Bakr, publicized by Uthman with unanimous agreement of the Companions, so denying any verse from it is kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted that any verse in the Quran you recite is exactly as revealed? How does the unanimous agreement of the Companions, as described in this lesson, remove that doubt? What would it mean for your faith to entertain such a doubt after knowing this?
?
The Rafidah claimed Ali compiled the Quran. Why does it matter to your personal relationship with Allah to know that the Quran was actually compiled by Abu Bakr and publicized by Uthman with full consensus? Does this historical truth strengthen your certainty in every letter of the Quran you read today?
?
Uthman faced disagreement but warned of greater future disagreement. When you encounter modern doubts about the Quran's authenticity, do you submit to the consensus of the Sahaba and the ummah, or do you follow your own reasoning? What is at stake for your soul if you reject even one verse from the mushaf that the Companions unanimously agreed upon?

The Revealed Scriptures: Their Number and Nature
Enumeration of the revealed scriptures and affirmation that they are created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the total number of revealed scriptures and the doctrinal position that they are the created speech of Allah.

Core Idea
All scriptures are created speech of Allah, numbering 100 scrolls and 4 books.

Imam Nasafi establishes a foundational truth: all revealed scriptures are the created speech of Allah. He then provides a detailed enumeration of the scriptures sent to various prophets, from Seth to Muhammad (peace be upon them). This lesson covers that enumeration and the key belief that the Qur'an and all previous books are created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about scriptures
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Notice the listing of prophets and the numbers of scriptures given to each.
Pay attention to the phrase 'kalam Allah makhluq'.
2
Identify the total count
The passage states there are 100 scrolls and 4 books. Count the scrolls assigned to each prophet to verify the total.
The scrolls sum to 50+30+10+10=100.
3
Understand 'created speech'
Consider why the author emphasizes that the scriptures are created. This is a key Ash'ari and Maturidi position against the view that the Qur'an is eternal.
This will be important for later lessons on the nature of the Qur'an.
4
Reflect on the sequence
Notice the chronological order: from Seth to Muhammad, with the final two prophets being Isa (last of Bani Israel) and Muhammad (last of all messengers).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكلام على الكتب المنزلة
يجب أن تعرف أن جميع الكتب التي أنزلها الله تعالى على الأنبياء والرسل كلام الله مخلوق، وذلك مئة صحيفة وأربعة كتب خمسون منها أنزلها الله تعالى على شيث بن آدم عليهما السلام، وثلاثون صحيفة على إدريس صلوات الله عليه، وعشر مصاحف على إبراهيم عليه السلام، وعشر مصاحف على موسى عليه السلام قبل نزول التوراة وتسمى كتاب الستة، وكان قبل غرق فرعون عليه اللعنة.
ثم أنزل الله تعالى التوراة بعد غرق فرعون، ثم أنزل الله تعالى الزبور على داود عليه السلام، ثم أنزل الله تعالى الإنجيل على عيسى عليه السلام، وهو آخر الأنبياء من بني إسرائيل، ثم أنزل الفرقان على محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو آخر الرسل.
فكل من أنكر آيةً من هذه الكتب فإنه يكفر، وإذا قال: آمنت بجميع الرسل ثم أنكر واحدًا من الرسل الذي ليس بمنصوص عليه، وقال: هذا ليس منهم لا يكفر ويكون مبتدعاً، هذا إذا لم يدخل في دين من الأديان، أما إذا دخل في دين من الأديان يكون مرتداً فيقتل.
والدليل على أن الإيمان بجميع الكتب شرط قال الله تعالى: ﴿ قُولُوا آمَنَّا بِاللَّهِ وَمَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْنَا ﴾ [البقرة: ١٣٦] الآية والإيمان بجميع الرسل شرط، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَلَكِنَّ الْبِرَّ مَنْ آمَنَ بِاللَّهِ وَالْيَوْمِ الْآخِرِ وَالْمَلَائِكَةِ وَالْكِتَابِ وَالنَّبِيِّينَ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٧٧].
Section on the discourse concerning the revealed books. You must know that all the books that Allah the Exalted sent down upon the prophets and messengers are the created speech of Allah. That is one hundred scrolls and four books: fifty of them Allah sent down upon Sheth the son of Adam (peace be upon them both), thirty scrolls upon Idris (peace be upon him), ten scriptures upon Ibrahim (peace be upon him), and ten scriptures upon Musa (peace be upon him) before the revelation of the Torah, and they are called the 'Book of the Six,' and that was before the drowning of Pharaoh (may the curse be upon him). Then Allah sent down the Torah after the drowning of Pharaoh, then Allah sent down the Zabur upon Dawud (peace be upon him), then Allah sent down the Injil upon Isa (peace be upon him), and he is the last of the prophets of the Children of Israel, then He sent down the Furqan upon Muhammad (may Allah bless him and grant him peace), and he is the last of the messengers. Whoever denies a single verse from these books becomes a disbeliever. And if someone says, 'I believe in all the messengers,' but then denies one of the messengers who is not explicitly named, and says, 'This one is not among them,' he does not become a disbeliever but becomes an innovator. This is if he has not entered into any religion. But if he has entered into a religion, he becomes an apostate and is to be killed. The proof that belief in all the books is a condition: Allah the Exalted said, 'Say, "We believe in Allah and what has been revealed to us..."' [Al-Baqarah 2:136]—the verse. And belief in all the messengers is a condition: Allah the Exalted said, 'But righteousness is [in] one who believes in Allah, the Last Day, the angels, the Book, and the prophets' [Al-Baqarah 2:177].
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on the revealed books (al-kutub al-munazzala). It begins by affirming that all scriptures sent down by Allah to prophets and messengers are created speech (kalam Allah makhluq), a key Ash'ari and Maturidi position that the Qur'an is created. The author then provides a detailed enumeration of the total scriptures: 100 scrolls (suhuf) and 4 books. These are distributed as 50 scrolls to Seth (Shith), 30 to Idris (Enoch), 10 to Ibrahim, and 10 to Musa before the Torah, collectively called 'Kitab al-Sittah' (the Book of the Six) — though the exact meaning is unclear and may refer to a collection. Then the four major books are listed: Torah to Musa, Zabur (Psalms) to Dawud, Injil (Gospel) to Isa — who is the last prophet of the Children of Israel — and finally the Furqan (Qur'an) to Muhammad (s), the last of the messengers. The text then states a firm ruling: anyone who denies a single verse from any of these books becomes a disbeliever (kafir). However, a more nuanced ruling follows: if a person claims to believe in all messengers but denies a specific messenger who is not explicitly mentioned by name in revelation, he does not become a kafir but becomes an innovator (mubtadi'). This applies only if he has not entered into a particular religion (i.e., he is not a convert). If he had entered a religion and then denies a prophet, he becomes an apostate (murtadd) and is executed. Finally, the author provides Quranic proofs that belief in all books and all messengers is a condition of iman, citing verses 2:136 and 2:177.

Key Terms
الكتب المنزلة
كلام الله مخلوق
التوراة
الزبور
الإنجيل
الفرقان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think only the Qur'an is Allah's speech, and previous books are not His speech or are not preserved.
Correction: This passage establishes that all scriptures are Allah's created speech, and they are all to be believed in.

Checkpoint
How many scrolls were sent to Prophet Idris according to Imam Nasafi?
A) 50
B) 30
C) 10
D) 4

Key Takeaway
All revealed scriptures, totaling 100 scrolls and 4 books, are the created speech of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that all scriptures are created speech of Allah. When you recite the Qur'an, do you truly feel you are handling something created, or do you unconsciously treat it as if it were eternal? What needs to change in your heart so that you revere it as the created word of Allah?
?
You have been given the final scripture after many earlier revelations. How seriously do you take the responsibility of being a recipient of the Furqan? What is one concrete action you will take this week to show that you believe this book is Allah's speech to you?
?
The passage lists prophets who received scriptures, from Sheth to Muhammad. Which of these prophets do you know the least about? How can learning about his life and struggles deepen your connection to the scripture given to him, and ultimately to the Qur'an?

Rulings on Denying Scriptures and Unnamed Prophets
Rulings on denial of scriptures and unnamed prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rulings: denial of any verse from the revealed books leads to kufr, and denial of a non-explicitly named prophet leads to bid'ah or apostasy depending on prior religious affiliation.

Core Idea
Denial of any revealed verse is kufr; denial of an unnamed prophet is bid'ah, not kufr.

After listing the scriptures, Imam Nasafi turns to the practical implications of belief. He gives a strict ruling that rejecting any part of these books is disbelief. He then addresses the case of someone who denies a prophet not explicitly named in revelation—a nuanced distinction between kufr and bid'ah, and when it becomes apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Read the rulings passage
Read the Arabic about the rulings. Notice the two scenarios: denial of a verse and denial of a non-explicit prophet.
The word 'mubtadi'' appears for the first time.
2
Understand the first ruling
Whoever denies any verse from these books becomes a kafir. Why do you think it's so severe?
Because it rejects divine revelation.
3
Analyze the second ruling
If someone denies a prophet not explicitly named in the Quran, he becomes a mubtadi' (innovator), not a kafir. But note the condition: only if he hasn't entered any religion.
If he had entered a religion, he becomes a murtadd (apostate) and is executed.
4
Compare the two
Why is denying a named book different from denying an unnamed prophet? Consider the clarity of revelation.
The distinction shows the importance of explicit textual evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكلام على الكتب المنزلة
يجب أن تعرف أن جميع الكتب التي أنزلها الله تعالى على الأنبياء والرسل كلام الله مخلوق، وذلك مئة صحيفة وأربعة كتب خمسون منها أنزلها الله تعالى على شيث بن آدم عليهما السلام، وثلاثون صحيفة على إدريس صلوات الله عليه، وعشر مصاحف على إبراهيم عليه السلام، وعشر مصاحف على موسى عليه السلام قبل نزول التوراة وتسمى كتاب الستة، وكان قبل غرق فرعون عليه اللعنة.

Section on the discourse concerning the revealed books. You must know that all the books that Allah the Exalted sent down upon the prophets and messengers are the created speech of Allah. That is one hundred scrolls and four books: fifty of them Allah sent down upon Sheth the son of Adam (peace be upon them both), thirty scrolls upon Idris (peace be upon him), ten scriptures upon Ibrahim (peace be upon him), and ten scriptures upon Musa (peace be upon him) before the revelation of the Torah, and they are called the 'Book of the Six,' and that was before the drowning of Pharaoh (may the curse be upon him). Quranic Proofs for Belief in All Books and Messengers
Quranic proofs that belief in all books and messengers is a condition of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic evidence that belief in all revealed books and all messengers is a condition of iman.

Core Idea
The Quran itself commands belief in all scriptures and messengers as a condition of righteousness.

Imam Nasafi concludes this section by providing direct Quranic evidence for the necessity of believing in all revealed books and all messengers. He cites two verses from Surah al-Baqarah that link true faith to belief in the entirety of revelation and all prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof verses
Read the Arabic passage with the two Quranic verses. Notice how they are introduced as proof (al-dalil).
The verses are from Surah al-Baqarah: 2:136 and 2:177.
2
Analyze the first proof
Verse 2:136 commands believers to affirm belief in Allah and what has been revealed to them and to previous prophets. What is the effect of saying 'we believe in all'?
It makes belief in all books a requirement.
3
Analyze the second proof
Verse 2:177 defines righteousness as belief in Allah, the Last Day, angels, the Book, and the prophets. How does this support belief in all messengers?
The mention of 'the Book' (singular) may encompass all scriptures, and 'the prophets' implies all.
4
Connect to earlier rulings
These proofs justify why denying any verse or any prophet is problematic. They establish the obligation of universal belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكلام على الكتب المنزلة
يجب أن تعرف أن جميع الكتب التي أنزلها الله تعالى على الأنبياء والرسل كلام الله مخلوق، وذلك مئة صحيفة وأربعة كتب خمسون منها أنزلها الله تعالى على شيث بن آدم عليهما السلام، وثلاثون صحيفة على إدريس صلوات الله عليه، وعشر مصاحف على إبراهيم عليه السلام، وعشر مصاحف على موسى عليه السلام قبل نزول التوراة وتسمى كتاب الستة، وكان قبل غرق فرعون عليه اللعنة.
ثم أنزل الله تعالى التوراة بعد غرق فرعون، ثم أنزل الله تعالى الزبور على داود عليه السلام، ثم أنزل الله تعالى الإنجيل على عيسى عليه السلام، وهو آخر الأنبياء من بني إسرائيل، ثم أنزل الفرقان على محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو آخر الرسل.
فكل من أنكر آيةً من هذه الكتب فإنه يكفر، وإذا قال: آمنت بجميع الرسل ثم أنكر واحدًا من الرسل الذي ليس بمنصوص عليه، وقال: هذا ليس منهم لا يكفر ويكون مبتدعاً، هذا إذا لم يدخل في دين من الأديان، أما إذا دخل في دين من الأديان يكون مرتداً فيقتل.
والدليل على أن الإيمان بجميع الكتب شرط قال الله تعالى: ﴿ قُولُوا آمَنَّا بِاللَّهِ وَمَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْنَا ﴾ [البقرة: ١٣٦] الآية والإيمان بجميع الرسل شرط، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَلَكِنَّ الْبِرَّ مَنْ آمَنَ بِاللَّهِ وَالْيَوْمِ الْآخِرِ وَالْمَلَائِكَةِ وَالْكِتَابِ وَالنَّبِيِّينَ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٧٧].
Section on the discourse concerning the revealed books. You must know that all the books that Allah the Exalted sent down upon the prophets and messengers are the created speech of Allah. That is one hundred scrolls and four books: fifty of them Allah sent down upon Sheth the son of Adam (peace be upon them both), thirty scrolls upon Idris (peace be upon him), ten scriptures upon Ibrahim (peace be upon him), and ten scriptures upon Musa (peace be upon him) before the revelation of the Torah, and they are called the 'Book of the Six,' and that was before the drowning of Pharaoh (may the curse be upon him). Then Allah sent down the Torah after the drowning of Pharaoh, then Allah sent down the Zabur upon Dawud (peace be upon him), then Allah sent down the Injil upon Isa (peace be upon him), and he is the last of the prophets of the Children of Israel, then He sent down the Furqan upon Muhammad (may Allah bless him and grant him peace), and he is the last of the messengers. Whoever denies a single verse from these books becomes a disbeliever. And if someone says, 'I believe in all the messengers,' but then denies one of the messengers who is not explicitly named, and says, 'This one is not among them,' he does not become a disbeliever but becomes an innovator. This is if he has not entered into any religion. But if he has entered into a religion, he becomes an apostate and is to be killed. The proof that belief in all the books is a condition: Allah the Exalted said, 'Say, "We believe in Allah and what has been revealed to us..."' [Al-Baqarah 2:136]—the verse. And belief in all the messengers is a condition: Allah the Exalted said, 'But righteousness is [in] one who believes in Allah, the Last Day, the angels, the Book, and the prophets' [Al-Baqarah 2:177].
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on the revealed books (al-kutub al-munazzala). It begins by affirming that all scriptures sent down by Allah to prophets and messengers are created speech (kalam Allah makhluq), a key Ash'ari and Maturidi position that the Qur'an is created. The author then provides a detailed enumeration of the total scriptures: 100 scrolls (suhuf) and 4 books. These are distributed as 50 scrolls to Seth (Shith), 30 to Idris (Enoch), 10 to Ibrahim, and 10 to Musa before the Torah, collectively called 'Kitab al-Sittah' (the Book of the Six) — though the exact meaning is unclear and may refer to a collection. Then the four major books are listed: Torah to Musa, Zabur (Psalms) to Dawud, Injil (Gospel) to Isa — who is the last prophet of the Children of Israel — and finally the Furqan (Qur'an) to Muhammad (s), the last of the messengers. The text then states a firm ruling: anyone who denies a single verse from any of these books becomes a disbeliever (kafir). However, a more nuanced ruling follows: if a person claims to believe in all messengers but denies a specific messenger who is not explicitly mentioned by name in revelation, he does not become a kafir but becomes an innovator (mubtadi'). This applies only if he has not entered into a particular religion (i.e., he is not a convert). If he had entered a religion and then denies a prophet, he becomes an apostate (murtadd) and is executed. Finally, the author provides Quranic proofs that belief in all books and all messengers is a condition of iman, citing verses 2:136 and 2:177.

Key Terms
الدليل
الإيمان بالكتاب
النبيين

D) 2:285

Key Takeaway
The Quran explicitly requires belief in all revealed books and all messengers as part of true faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The verse 2:177 defines righteousness as believing in all prophets and books. Have you ever doubted that a previous scripture like the Torah or Gospel was originally from Allah? If so, how does this lesson challenge you to correct that doubt?

Belief in all messengers includes believing in prophets like Hud, Salih, and Shu'ayb. Do you know their stories well enough to truly believe in them? Commit to learning about at least one of them this week.

Refuting the Claim that Naskh Implies Badā' in God
Nasafi refutes the charge that abrogation implies badā' and explains its true nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why abrogation (naskh) does not imply any change in God's knowledge or will, but rather reveals the temporary nature of the abrogated ruling.

Core Idea
Naskh reveals a hidden deadline; it does not change God's mind.

This lesson addresses a common objection against the Islamic concept of abrogation: that it implies badā' (alteration) in God. Nasafi explains that abrogation is simply the expiration of a temporary ruling, not a change in God's will. He uses an analogy with resurrection to clarify his point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument.
Pay attention to the objection and the answer structure.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the claim that naskh implies badā' and raj' in God, and why that is problematic.
Badā' and raj' are said to indicate ignorance of outcomes.
3
Grasp Nasafi's denial and redefinition
Notice how Nasafi denies the premise and redefines naskh as the termination of one ruling and commencement of another.
The first ruling was temporary from the start, but its term was unknown to humans.
4
Examine the analogy with resurrection
Look at the analogy: just as resurrection does not imply badā' but rather expiration of death's rule, so naskh is expiration of the abrogated ruling.
This shows that a change in apparent state does not require change in God's knowledge.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this analogy effectively refutes the objection and reinforces the orthodox Sunni position on naskh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبداء والرجوع من الله تعالى لا يصح؛ لأن البداء والرجوع إنما يكون ممن كان جاهلاً ولا يعرف عواقب الأمور. والجواب عنه أن نقول : لا نُسلّم بأن في النسخ بِدَاءً ورجوعًا، بل هو انقضاء حكم أوّل وانتهاؤه، واستئناف حكم آخر ؛ لأنه قد ظهر لنا أن الحكم الأول لم يكن مؤبدا، لكنه مؤقت إلى ذلك الوقت، إلا أنا لا نعرف ذلك، فظهر لنا أن الحكم الأول قد انتهى وانقضى. يدل عليه أنَّ الله تعالى يحشر الموتى يوم القيامة، ولا يقال بأن فيه بداءً ورجوعا، بل فيه انتهاء حكم الموتى، ويستأنف حكمًا آخر، كذلك ههنا ولا يقال بأن في النسخ بداءً ورجوعاً، بل فيه انتهاء حكم المنسوخ في الموتى، واستئناف حكم الناسخ.
Alteration and revocation do not apply to Allah the Exalted, because alteration and revocation only come from one who is ignorant and does not know the outcomes of affairs. The answer to this is that we say: we do not concede that in abrogation there is alteration and revocation. Rather, it is the termination of a first ruling and its expiration, and the commencement of another ruling; because it has become apparent to us that the first ruling was not permanent, but was temporary until that time, only we did not know that. So it became apparent to us that the first ruling has ended and expired. Evidence for this is that Allah the Exalted will resurrect the dead on the Day of Resurrection, and it is not said that in that there is alteration and revocation, but rather in it is the expiration of the rule of death and He commences another rule. Similarly here, it is not said that in abrogation there is alteration and revocation, but rather in it is the expiration of the abrogated ruling, as in [the case of] the dead, and the commencement of the abrogating ruling.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. The passage begins by stating an objection: that abrogation (naskh) implies badā' (alteration) and raj' (revocation) in God, which are impossible because they arise only from ignorance of outcomes. Nasafi then gives the answer: he first denies the premise that naskh involves badā' or raj'. Instead, naskh is simply the expiration of one ruling and the beginning of another. This is because the first ruling was not eternal but temporary, though humans were unaware of its time limit. When the limit arrives, it becomes known that the ruling has ended. He supports this with an analogy: God will resurrect the dead on Judgment Day. No one says that resurrection implies a change in God's will; rather, it is the natural expiration of the state of death and the start of a new state. Similarly, naskh is the expiration of the abrogated ruling and the start of the abrogating ruling—no change in God's knowledge or will occurs.

Key Terms
البداء
الرجوع
النسخ
انقضاء الحكم
استئناف الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Naskh means God changes His mind because He realizes a previous ruling was wrong.
Correction: Naskh is merely the revelation of a previously hidden time limit; the ruling was always temporary, so no change in God's knowledge or will occurs.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the true nature of naskh (abrogation) in relation to God?
A) A change in God's will due to new circumstances.
B) The expiration of a temporary ruling and commencement of another.
C) An indication of ignorance in God.
D) A revocation of a previous command out of mercy.

Key Takeaway
Naskh does not involve alteration in God; it is the expiration of a temporary ruling and the start of another, known to God from eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accused God of being inconsistent because a command changed? How does this passage challenge your assumption that you know the full timeline of His plan?
?
When you face a situation where an earlier ruling no longer applies (e.g., in fiqh), do you submit to the new ruling as part of God's perfect knowledge, or do you feel unsettled as if He changed His mind? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in His wisdom?
?
If you truly believed that God's knowledge encompasses all hidden deadlines, how would that change your urgency to obey His commands today, even the ones that seem temporary?

The Benefit of Abrogation: Mercy and Practical Wisdom
Nasafi answers the question about the benefit of naskh with examples of divine mercy and practical obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why abrogation (naskh) is beneficial according to Imam Nasafi, both as a mercy from Allah and as a practical necessity for updating obligations while preserving belief in earlier rulings.

Core Idea
Abrogation is a mercy that lightens burdens while keeping belief in earlier rulings intact.

One might wonder why Allah would change a command after revealing it—wouldn't that imply imperfection? Imam Nasafi answers this with a clear question-and-answer: abrogation benefits by showing tenderness and mercy, and it updates obligations to suit current circumstances while keeping belief in the original ruling intact. Let's read his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the question-and-answer format.
The Arabic includes Quranic verses that are part of the explanation.
2
Identify the two benefits mentioned
Find where Nasafi states the benefits: tenderness/alleviation/mercy, and the practical benefit of the abrogating ruling being immediately actionable.
The Quranic example illustrates the mercy aspect.
3
Analyze the Quranic example
Look at the verse quoted: initially one Muslim had to fight ten disbelievers, later lightened to one fighting two. Notice that Allah Himself calls it a 'lightening' (takhfif).
This shows that naskh is not arbitrary but a response to human weakness.
4
Distinguish between obligation to act and obligation to believe
Nasafi explains that the abrogating ruling requires immediate action and belief, while the abrogated ruling still requires belief but not action.
This answers the potential objection that abrogation makes earlier rulings useless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: أيش الفائدة في النسخ؟ قلنا: الفائدة في النسخ التحنن والتخفيف والرحمة على عباده، كما أن الله تعالى أمر المسلمين في الابتداء بأن يقاتل منهم كل واحد العشرة من الكفرة والفجرة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وإن يكُن مِّنكُمْ عِشْرُونَ صَبِرُونَ يَغْلِبُوا مِائَتَيْنِ ﴾ ثم خفف بعد ذلك وأسقط عن كل عشرة ثمانية بقوله تعالى: ﴿ الْآنَ خَفَّفَ اللَّهُ عَنكُمْ وَعَلِمَ أَنَّ فِيكُمْ ضَعْفًا ﴾ سماه تخفيفا، كذلك ههنا الناسخ أنفع في الحال؛ لأنه يوجب العمل به في الحال والإيمان به واجب والمنسوخ لا يجب العمل به في الحال، ولكن يجب الإيمان به.
If it is said: What is the benefit in abrogation? We say: The benefit in abrogation is tenderness and alleviation and mercy upon His servants, just as Allah the Exalted commanded the Muslims initially that each one of them fight ten of the disbelievers and wicked people by His saying: 'If there be twenty steadfast among you, they shall overcome two hundred.' Then He lightened afterwards and removed from every ten eight by His saying: 'Now Allah has lightened your burden, for He knows that there is weakness in you.' He called it lightening. Similarly here, the abrogating ruling is more beneficial in the present time because it necessitates acting upon it immediately, and belief in it is obligatory, while the abrogated ruling does not necessitate acting upon it now, but belief in it is obligatory.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer format. It begins with a question: What is the benefit (fā'ida) of naskh? The answer gives two benefits: tenderness (tahanun) and alleviation (takhfīf) and mercy (rahma) upon the servants. Then Nasafi supports this with a scriptural example: initially, Muslims were commanded that one man fight ten disbelievers (Qur'an 8:65). Later, this was lightened so that one fights two (Qur'an 8:66). God Himself called this 'takhfīf' (lightening). So naskh is a mercy. Nasafi then adds another benefit: the abrogating ruling is more beneficial in the present moment because it obligates immediate action and belief in it is required. The abrogated ruling does not require current action, but belief in it remains obligatory. Thus, naskh preserves the obligation of belief while updating practical obligations to suit the community's capacity.

Key Terms
النسخ
التحنن
التخفيف
الرحمة
الناسخ
المنسوخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that abrogation renders the abrogated ruling false or useless.
Correction: The passage clarifies that belief in the abrogated ruling remains obligatory, even though action is no longer required. Thus abrogation does not nullify the earlier revelation's truth.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi cite as a scriptural example of the benefit of abrogation?
A) The change of the qibla from Jerusalem to Mecca.
B) The reduction of the required number of fighters from ten to two per opponent.
C) The abrogation of the prohibition of fighting in sacred months.
D) The permission to break the fast during travel.

Key Takeaway
For Nasafi, abrogation is a mercy from Allah that lightens obligations and is immediately beneficial for the present community, while belief in the abrogated ruling remains obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a change in your religious practice or understanding, do you instinctively resist it as a loss, or can you consider that it might be a mercy from Allah tailored to your current weakness? How would your life look if you trusted that every divine adjustment is an act of tenderness?

The passage emphasizes that belief in abrogated rulings remains obligatory. Are there teachings from the past that you have overlooked or dismissed because they seem irrelevant today? What is one specific ruling you can revere as part of Allah’s revelation, even if you no longer practice it?

Nasafi highlights that the abrogating ruling is immediately beneficial and demands action. Is there a command from Allah that you are postponing, thinking you are not ready? How does knowing that the command itself is an alleviation and mercy motivate you to act on it today?

The Permissibility of Abrogation in Sharia
Nasafi refutes the Jewish claim that abrogation is impossible by using analogies of food, drink, and medicine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna holds that abrogation is permissible and how the Jewish objection is refuted using analogies from daily life.

Core Idea
Divine abrogation is not caprice but wisdom adapted to time.

This lesson addresses a key objection raised by the Jews against the concept of abrogation (naskh) in divine law. The objection claims that abrogation implies God commanded harm, which is incompatible with His wisdom. Imam Nasafi presents the Sunni response using simple analogies from daily life—food, drink, and medicine—showing that changing commands according to time reflects mercy, not foolishness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the confident tone in the response.
2
Identify the Jewish objection
Find the specific reasoning the Jews give for why abrogation is impossible. Underline the logical steps in the translation.
The key premise: commands imply benefit, prohibitions imply harm.
3
Examine the analogies in the response
Nasafi uses two analogies: food/drink and a physician. For each, ask: what point does it illustrate about timing and benefit?
The physician analogy is particularly strong because it compares God's mercy to that of a caring doctor.
4
Connect to the sequence of scriptures
Notice how the response concludes by listing the Torah, Psalms, Gospel, and Qur'an as each being beneficial for their eras. This shows that abrogation is not arbitrary but part of a divine plan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في وقوع النسخ في الشريعة. قالت اليهود [ لعنهم الله ] : نَسْخُ الشريعة لا يجوز. وعند أهل السنة والجماعة يجوز. واحتجوا وقالوا لأن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي المصلحة، والنهي عن الشيء يقتضي المفسدة، وإذا كان كذلك فالله تعالى لما أمر في التوراة ونهى، دلّ ذلك على أنه مصلحة، فلو جاز أن ينهى ما أمر به في التوراة يؤدي إلى أن الله تعالى أمر في التوراة بالمفسدة وهذا لا يجوز؛ لأن الله حكيم عالم بعواقب الأمور، ولا يجوز أن يوصف فعله بالسَّفَه. والجواب عنه قلنا: لأن الله تعالى إذا أمر بأمر يقتضي المصلحة في وقت، ولا يقتضي المصلحة في جميع الأوقات كالطعام والشراب يقتضي مصلحة في حالة الجوع، ولا يقتضي مصلحة في حالة الشَّبَعِ، وكالطبيب يأمر المريض بأدوية مختلفة في أوقات مختلفة، ولا يكون ذلك بداء، بل لتحقق المصلحة في ذلك الوقت، كذلك هنا الله تعالى أرحم على عباده من الطبيب الشفيق، وحين جعل التوراة شريعة في زمن موسى عليه السلام؛ كان ذلك مصلحة إلى انقضاء زمن موسى عليه السلام، ثم صارت المصلحة في الزبور إلى انقضاء زمن داود عليه السلام، ثم صارت المصلحة في الإنجيل إلى انقضاء زمن عيسى عليه السلام، ثم صارت المصلحة في القرآن في عصر نبينا محمد .
Chapter on the occurrence of abrogation in the Sharia. The Jews — may Allah curse them — said: Abrogation of the Sharia is not permissible. According to Ahl al-Sunna wa'l-Jama'a, it is permissible. They argued saying: Because commanding a thing necessitates benefit, and forbidding a thing necessitates harm. And if that is so, then when Allah commanded in the Torah and forbade, that indicates it was a benefit. So if it were permissible that He forbid what He commanded in the Torah, it would lead to Allah having commanded harm in the Torah, and that is not permissible, because Allah is Wise, Knowing of the outcomes of affairs, and it is not permissible that His action be described as foolishness. The answer to this: We say: Because Allah the Exalted, when He commands a command, it necessitates benefit at [that] time, but does not necessitate benefit at all times, like food and drink: they necessitate benefit in a state of hunger, but do not necessitate benefit in a state of fullness. And like the physician: he prescribes different medicines to the patient at different times, and that is not alteration, but rather for the realization of benefit at that time. Similarly here, Allah is more merciful to His servants than the compassionate physician. And when He made the Torah a Sharia in the time of Moses — peace be upon him — that was a benefit until the expiration of the time of Moses. Then the benefit became in the Psalms until the expiration of the time of David. Then the benefit became in the Gospel until the expiration of the time of Jesus. Then the benefit became in the Qur'an in the era of our Prophet Muhammad.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a classification (chapter) containing an objection-and-answer argument. It begins with a chapter heading on the occurrence of abrogation in Sharia. The Jews argue that abrogation is impermissible because commands imply benefit and prohibitions imply harm; if God later forbade what He previously commanded in the Torah, it would mean He commanded something harmful, which is incompatible with His wisdom and knowledge. The Sunni response uses analogies: food and drink are beneficial when hungry but harmful when full; a doctor changes prescriptions over time without it implying foolishness (safah). Similarly, divine laws are suited to their eras: Torah for Moses' time, Psalms for David's, Gospel for Jesus', and Qur'an for Muhammad's. Abrogation is not a change of mind (badā') but a change in what is beneficial as time changes, and God is more merciful than any physician.

Key Terms
النسخ
المصلحة
المفسدة
السفه
الشريعة
الزبور
الإنجيل
القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think abrogation means God changed His mind or made a mistake, implying divine foolishness.
Correction: The passage clarifies that abrogation is based on time-conditioned benefit, just as food is beneficial only when hungry. God's commands are perfectly wise for each period, and changing them reflects mercy, not error.

Checkpoint
What analogies does Nasafi use to refute the Jewish claim that abrogation implies God commanded harm?
A) A teacher and student; a king and his subjects.
B) Food and drink; a physician and patient.
C) A builder and a house; a farmer and crops.
D) A parent and child; a judge and litigants.

Key Takeaway
Abrogation is permissible because divine commands are conditioned by time and circumstance, as illustrated by analogies of food and medicine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you face a situation where what was once allowed is now forbidden (or vice versa), do you submit to God's new command, or do you cling to the old because it was 'better'? What would it take for you to trust His timing as the compassionate Physician?
?
This passage says God is 'more merciful than the compassionate physician.' Is there an area of your obedience where you doubt His mercy because you don't understand His prescription? What step can you take today to trust Him more deeply?

The Permissibility of Abrogation in the Sharia
Imam Nasafi presents and refutes the Jewish objection to abrogation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jewish objection to abrogation and how Imam Nasafi refutes it using the principle that benefit is relative to time and circumstance.

Core Idea
Abrogation reflects divine wisdom adapting to changing times, not a change in God's mind.

This passage tackles a key objection raised by the Jewish tradition against the concept of abrogation (naskh) in Islamic law. Imam Nasafi presents the Jewish argument that abrogation would imply God commanded harm, then refutes it with clear reasoning and analogies from everyday life. By the end, you will see how abrogation is consistent with divine wisdom and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify positions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two opposing views: the Jewish denial of abrogation and the Sunni affirmation.
The passage begins with the Jewish claim and then states the Sunni position.
2
Analyze the Jewish argument
Understand why the Jews argue abrogation is impossible: every command implies benefit, so changing it would mean God originally commanded harm, which is unworthy of a wise God.
The argument rests on the assumption that benefit is intrinsic and unchanging.
3
Examine the refutation: benefit is time-relative
Nasafi uses the analogy of food and drink: beneficial when hungry, harmful when full. Similarly, divine commands can be beneficial at one time and not another.
The physician analogy reinforces this: different treatments for different stages of illness.
4
Connect to historical scriptures
Nasafi applies the principle to the succession of revealed books: Torah for Moses, Psalms for David, Gospel for Jesus, and Quran for Muhammad—each suited to its time.
This shows abrogation is part of a consistent divine plan.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Conclude that abrogation does not imply a change in God's knowledge or wisdom, but a change in what benefits humanity at different times.
This answers the common misconception that abrogation means God 'changed His mind.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في وقوع النسخ في الشريعة] قالت اليهود [ لعنهم الله ](١) : نَسْخُ الشريعة لا يجوز. وعند أهل السنة والجماعة يجوز. واحتجوا وقالوا لأن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي المصلحة، والنهي عن الشيء يقتضي المفسدة، وإذا كان كذلك فالله تعالى لما أمر في التوراة ونهى، دلّ ذلك على أنه مصلحة، فلو جاز أن ينهى ما أمر به في التوراة يؤدي إلى أن الله تعالى أمر في التوراة بالمفسدة وهذا لا يجوز؛ لأن الله حكيم عالم بعواقب الأمور، ولا يجوز أن يوصف فعله بالسَّفَه. والجواب عنه قلنا: لأن الله تعالى إذا أمر بأمر يقتضي المصلحة في وقت، ولا يقتضي المصلحة في جميع الأوقات كالطعام والشراب يقتضي مصلحة في حالة الجوع، ولا يقتضي مصلحة في حالة الشَّبَعِ، وكالطبيب يأمر المريض بأدوية مختلفة في أوقات مختلفة، ولا يكون ذلك بداء، بل لتحقق المصلحة في ذلك الوقت، كذلك هنا الله تعالى أرحم على عباده من الطبيب الشفيق، وحين جعل التوراة شريعة في زمن موسى عليه السلام؛ كان ذلك مصلحة إلى انقضاء زمن موسى عليه السلام، ثم صارت المصلحة في الزبور إلى انقضاء زمن داود عليه السلام، ثم صارت المصلحة في الإنجيل إلى انقضاء زمن عيسى عليه السلام، ثم صارت المصلحة في القرآن في عصر نبينا محمد .
Chapter on the occurrence of abrogation in the Sharia. The Jews (may Allah curse them) said: Abrogation of the Sharia is not permissible. And according to Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, it is permissible. They argued and said: Because commanding a thing necessitates benefit, and forbidding a thing necessitates harm; and if that is so, then when Allah Most High commanded and forbade in the Torah, that indicates that it was a benefit. If it were permissible that He forbid what He commanded in the Torah, it would lead to Allah Most High having commanded harm in the Torah, and that is not permissible because Allah is Wise, Knower of the outcomes of affairs, and it is not permissible that His action be described as foolishness. The answer to it: we say: Because Allah Most High, when He commands a thing, it necessitates benefit at a time, and it does not necessitate benefit at all times, like food and drink that necessitate benefit in the state of hunger, and do not necessitate benefit in the state of satiety; and like the physician who prescribes different medications to the patient at different times, and that is not due to illness, but rather to achieve benefit at that time. Similarly here, Allah Most High is more merciful to His servants than the compassionate physician. When He made the Torah a law in the time of Moses (peace be upon him), that was a benefit until the end of the time of Moses (peace be upon him); then the benefit became in the Psalms until the end of the time of David (peace be upon him); then the benefit became in the Gospel until the end of the time of Jesus (peace be upon him); then the benefit became in the Qur'an in the time of our Prophet Muhammad.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Jewish denial of abrogation (naskh). It begins by stating the Jewish claim that abrogation is not permissible, and contrasts it with the position of Ahl al-Sunna that it is permissible. Then the passage presents the Jewish argument: that every command of Allah entails benefit (maslaha) and every prohibition entails harm (mafsada); therefore, if Allah commanded something in the Torah, it must be intrinsically beneficial, and to later forbid it would imply that He had commanded harm originally—impossible for a wise God. The answer is given: benefit is relative to time and circumstance. Just as food and drink are beneficial when hungry but not when full, and a physician varies prescriptions according to the patient's condition, Allah's commands are tailored to specific times and peoples. The Torah was beneficial for the time of Moses, the Psalms for David, the Gospel for Jesus, and the Qur'an for Muhammad (peace be upon them all). Thus, abrogation does not imply a change in God's wisdom but a change in what is beneficial for humanity.

Key Terms
نسخ
مصلحة
مفسدة
حكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation implies that God changed His mind or made a mistake.
Correction: The passage clarifies that abrogation reflects changing circumstances, not a change in God's knowledge or wisdom.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given by Imam Nasafi to refute the Jewish objection to abrogation?
A) Allah's commands are eternal and cannot be changed.
B) Benefit is relative to time and circumstance, like food and medicine.
C) The Torah was never a true revelation from Allah.
D) Abrogation only applies to non-legal matters.

Key Takeaway
Abrogation is not a contradiction in God's will but a manifestation of His wisdom in guiding humanity through different times.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever resist the idea that Allah's commands can change with time, clinging to a rigid interpretation of earlier rulings? How does this lesson challenge your comfort with fixed religious forms? Will you submit to the wisdom that what was best for the past may not be best for now—and that this does not diminish Allah's perfection?
?
Think of a personal habit or belief you've held as 'always right' for you. Could it be that your current stage of life requires a different 'prescription'? If Allah, the Most Merciful, adjusts His laws for entire communities, how much more should you be willing to adjust your own practices as your circumstances change? What step will you take today to re-evaluate one such fixed assumption?
?
The Jews rejected abrogation because they thought it implied foolishness in Allah. Are there any aspects of your faith where you secretly fear that a change in your understanding would imply God made a mistake? How can the analogy of the physician help you trust that all of Allah's commands—past and present—are perfectly wise for their time? Will you commit to trusting His guidance for your current time, even if it contradicts what you once believed was absolute?

Refutation of Mut'ah Marriage Permissibility
Imam Nasafi refutes the Rawafid claim that mut'ah marriage is perpetually permitted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna declare mut'ah marriage forbidden and how Quranic verses used by the Rawafid are abrogated.

Core Idea
Mut'ah is forbidden like wine; its temporary permission was abrogated.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a sectarian disagreement regarding mut'ah (temporary marriage). The Rawafid claim it remains permissible based on Quran 4:24. Nasafi presents the Sunni position that it was only temporarily allowed and later abrogated by other verses. Understanding this refutation is key to grasping the Sunni stance on marriage and the principle of naskh (abrogation) in Quranic rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two opposing views
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the Rawafid use Quran 4:24 as proof, while Ahl al-Sunna base their ruling on other verses.
The Rawafid argue that payment for enjoyment alone suffices, without a proper marriage contract.
2
Examine the Rawafid proof and the Sunni counter-evidence
The Rawafid rely on verse 4:24. The Sunnis counter that this verse is abrogated by verses 4:3 and 24:32, which command proper marriage, and by verse 24:2, which prescribes punishment for fornication, implying any sexual relationship outside marriage is illicit.
The principle of naskh (abrogation) is central: later revelations can supersede earlier ones.
3
Understand the concept of abrogation as applied here
Note that Ahl al-Sunna acknowledge mut'ah was once permitted in a specific journey out of necessity, but that permission was later abrogated. Reflect on why the Rawafid interpretation is rejected.
The abrogating verses establish marriage as the only permissible framework for sexual relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الرد على من قال بإباحة زواج المتعة] وصنف من الروافض قالوا بأن المتعة حلالٌ (۱)، وهو استئجار المرأة للوطء. قال الله تعالى: (فَمَا اسْتَمْتَعْتُم بِهِ مِنْهُنَّ فَكَاتُوهُنَّ أُجُورَهُنَّ فَرِيضَةً ) [النساء: ٢٤] أوجب الأجرة بمجرد الاستمتاع دون النكاح. وقال أهل السنة والجماعة المتعة حرام كالخمر، إلا أنها أبيحت في سَفَرٍ واحد للضرورة ثم نُسِخَتْ بقوله تعالى: ﴿ الزَّانِيَةُ وَالزَّانِي فَاجْلِدُوا كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مِائَةَ جَلْدَةٍ وَلَا تَأْخُذَكُر بيهما رأْفَةٌ فِي دِينِ اللَّهِ إِن كُنتُمْ تُؤْمِنُونَ بِاللَّهِ وَالْيَوْمِ الْآخِرِ وَلْيَشْهَدْ عَذَابَهُمَا طَائِفَةٌ مِّنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ ﴾ [النور: ٢]. وأما الآية قلنا: الآية نُسِخَتْ بقوله تعالى: [ فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاءِ ) [النساء: (٢) وَأَنكِحُوا الْأَيْمَى مِنكُمْ﴾ [النور: ٣٢].
Chapter on refuting those who say temporary marriage is permissible. A group of the Rawafid said that mut'ah is permissible, and it is hiring a woman for intercourse. Allah Most High said: 'So for whatever you have enjoyed from them, give them their due compensation as an obligation' (4:24). They argued that this verse obligates payment merely for enjoyment without marriage. Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: Mut'ah is forbidden like wine, except that it was permitted in one journey out of necessity, then it was abrogated by His statement: 'The fornicatress and the fornicator, flog each one of them with a hundred lashes...' (24:2). As for the verse (4:24), we say: it was abrogated by His statement: 'Marry what is lawful for you of women' (4:3) and 'Marry the unmarried among you' (24:32).
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Rawafid (a Shi'a sect) who claim that mut'ah (temporary marriage) is perpetually permissible. The Rawafid base their argument on Quran 4:24, which they interpret as permitting sexual enjoyment with payment without the need for a permanent marriage contract. The position of Ahl al-Sunna is that mut'ah is forbidden, analogous to wine. They acknowledge that it was temporarily permitted during one specific journey due to necessity, but that permission was later abrogated. Two Quranic verses are cited as evidence for the abrogation: 24:2 (the punishment for adultery) which implies that any sexual relationship outside a proper marriage is fornication, and 4:3 and 24:32 which command marriage in a proper framework. Thus, the verse the Rawafid rely on (4:24) is itself abrogated by later revelations.

Key Takeaway

Refutation of Re-created Bodies
Nasafi refutes Rawafid re-creation claim using Quran 4:56.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Nasafi's refutation of the Rawafid belief that after death Allah creates an entirely new body for the soul, and how he reinterprets Quran 4:56 as describing transformation, not replacement.

Core Idea
Bodies are transformed in form, not replaced in essence.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a subgroup of the Rawafid (Shi'a) who hold that when a person dies and decays, Allah creates a completely new body for the soul, likening the body to a cloak that can be swapped. They cite Quran 4:56, which speaks of replacing skins in Hellfire. Nasafi refutes this by explaining that the verse refers to a change in the skins' forms and qualities, not their essential substance. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for bodily resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice how the Rawafid view and their proof are presented first, then Nasafi's refutation.
The passage is cut off at the end; the second verse reference is incomplete.
2
Identify the Rawafid claim and evidence
Find the statement that describes the Rawafid belief: after death, Allah creates a new body for the soul. Note their analogy (body is like a cloak) and their proof from Quran 4:56.
Their proof relies on a literal reading of 'replace their skins with other skins'.
3
Understand Nasafi's counter-interpretation
Nasafi says the verse means a change in the skins' forms and qualities (hay'aatiha wa sifaatiha), not a change of their essence ('ayniha). This preserves the identity of the body while allowing for renewal.
He argues that the same skin continues to exist but its properties are altered.
4
Note the incomplete second verse
The passage begins to cite another verse ('On the Day...') to support this interpretation, but the source is cut off. Reflect on how a second verse would likely emphasize continuity of identity.
The missing verse probably shows that the same bodies are raised, not new ones.

وصنف منهم قالوا: إذا مات الرجل وصار رميمًا يخلق الله تعالى له جسدا آخر تدخل فيه الروح، وقالوا بأن الجسد للروح كالجبة للبدن، واحتجوا بقوله تعالى: (كلما نَضِجَتْ جُلُودُهُم بَدَّلْنَهُمْ جُلُودًا غَيْرَهَا لِيَذُوقُوا الْعَذَابَ ﴾ [النساء : ٥٦]. قلنا: أراد به تبديل هيئاتها وصفاتها، لا تبديل عينها، يدل عليه قوله تعالى: ﴿ يَوْمَ

They argued with His statement: 'Whenever their skins are cooked thoroughly, We replace them with other skins so that they may taste the punishment' (4:56). We said: He intended by it the change of their forms and qualities, not the change of their essence. This is indicated by His statement: 'On the Day...'
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a subgroup of the Rawafid who believe that after death and complete decay, Allah creates an entirely new body for the soul, comparing the body to a cloak that can be replaced. They cite Quran 4:56, which speaks of skin replacement in Hell, as evidence. The refutation states that the verse refers to a change in the form and qualities of the skin, not its essential substance. The point is that the same body is transformed, not replaced with a different one. The argument is supported by another verse (cut off) that indicates continuity of identity. Thus, the Rawafid's literal interpretation is incorrect; the skins remain the same in essence but are renewed in condition.

Change of Earth and Heavens: Attributes, Not Essence
Interpretive clarification of Qur'an 14:48 on change of earth and heavens

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Qur'anic description of the earth and heavens being changed refers to a transformation in their attributes rather than a replacement of their substance.

Core Idea
The earth and heavens will change in quality, not in essence.

This lesson examines a key interpretive principle from Imam Nasafi's Bahr al-Kalam regarding Qur'an 14:48. The author clarifies that the 'change' of the earth and heavens is a change of attributes, not a change of the very essence. This subtle distinction guards against misunderstanding the eschatological transformation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation. Notice how the author cites the verse and immediately provides his interpretation.
The Arabic includes a bracket around [كذلك ههنا] indicating an editorial addition.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between 'تبديل الصفة' (change of attribute) and 'تبديل العين' (change of essence). Understand this pair.
3
Consider the context
The phrase 'كذلك ههنا' (likewise here) suggests this principle applies to the ongoing discussion about resurrection or eschatology.
4
Reflect on the implication
If only attributes change but essence remains, what does that mean for continuity of creation? This interpretation balances divine power with consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبدلُ الْأَرْضُ غَيْرَ الْأَرْضِ وَالسَّمَوَاتُ وَبَرَزُوا لِلَّهِ الْوَاحِدِ الْقَهَّارِ ) [إبراهيم: ٤٨] وأراد به تبديل صفتها لا تبديل عينها [ كذلك ههنا ].

Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a brief interpretive note on Qur'an 14:48. The author clarifies that the 'change' of the earth and heavens mentioned in the verse refers to a transformation in their attributes (sifa) rather than a replacement of their very substance (ayn). The phrase 'likewise here' (kadhalika huna) indicates that this principle applies to the current discussion, likely a continuation from a preceding argument about resurrection or eschatological events. The author thus guards against a literal annihilationist reading, affirming continuity of essence with change in qualities.

Key Takeaway

?

What have you done today to ensure that your essential self—your heart and deeds—is prepared for that encounter? Will you face Him with a heart turned toward Him, or with attachment to fleeting attributes that will vanish?
?
The author insists that creation undergoes transformation, not annihilation. In your own life, where do you need a genuine transformation of your core being (your intentions and beliefs) rather than just a surface-level change in behavior? Sanctity of Muslim Property and Proofs
Imam Nasafi states the inviolability of Muslim property with Quran and hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic ruling on the inviolability of a Muslim's property and the scriptural evidence supporting it.

Core Idea
A Muslim's property is sacred and cannot be taken without willing consent.

Imam al-Nasafi begins with a clear doctrinal statement: a Muslim's property is not lawful to take except with the owner's willing consent. This principle is foundational for Islamic ethics and legal transactions. He then provides two key evidences from the Quran and Hadith to establish this rule. Let's read the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the direct statement followed by evidence.
2
Identify the ruling and its condition
Find the phrase 'لا يحل مال امرئ مسلم إلا بطيبة من نفسه' and understand its meaning.
The word 'طِيبَة' means willing consent or goodwill.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the verse cited and see how it supports the ruling by prohibiting unjust consumption and permitting trade by mutual consent.
Note the phrase 'تجارة عن تراض' (trade by mutual consent).
4
Examine the Hadith evidence
Note the hadith about burden of proof and oath, and how it protects property rights in disputes.
This hadith establishes a legal procedure.
5
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this principle is so important in Islamic law and daily life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أهل السنة والجماعة كثرهم الله تعالى إلى يوم القيامة: لا يَحِلُّ مالُ امري مسلم إلا بطيبة من نفسه. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا لَا تَأْكُلُوا أَمْوَالَكُم بَيْنَكُم بِالْبَاطِلِ إلَّا أَن تَكُونَ تِجَرَةٌ عَن تَرَاضِ مِنكُمْ ﴾ [النساء: ۲۹] والأحاديث الواردة في هذا الباب كثيرة منها قوله عليه السلام: البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر.
And Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama‘ah (may Allah multiply them until the Day of Resurrection) said: The property of a Muslim man is not lawful except with his own goodwill. Allah Almighty said: {O you who have believed, do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly unless it be a transaction by mutual consent} [al-Nisa 4:29]. And the hadiths reported on this topic are many, among them his statement, peace be upon him: 'The burden of proof is upon the claimant, and the oath is upon the one who denies.'
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal ruling (ḥukm) concerning the inviolability of a Muslim's property. Imam al-Nasafi begins by affirming the position of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama‘ah: it is not lawful to take the property of a fellow Muslim except with his willing consent. He then supports this principle with two types of evidence. First, he cites the Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Nisa (4:29) which prohibits consuming wealth unjustly and permits trade based on mutual consent. Second, he refers to the abundant hadiths on this subject, quoting one specifically: 'The burden of proof is upon the claimant, and the oath is upon the one who denies.' This hadith establishes a legal procedure that protects property rights by requiring the plaintiff to provide evidence and the defendant to swear an oath. Together, these texts show that property rights are divinely ordained and safeguarded through both substantive law and procedural justice.

Key Terms
حِلّ
طِيبَة نَفْس
تراض
البيّنة
اليمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Once a transaction is called 'trade', any form of taking wealth is permissible.
Correction: The verse explicitly requires mutual consent (tarāḍin), so trade without genuine consent is also prohibited.

Checkpoint
What is the primary condition for taking a Muslim's property according to the passage?
A) By any means if the taker is in need
B) Only with the owner's willing consent
C) If the property is left unattended
D) With permission from the ruler

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's property is sacred and may only be taken with the owner's willing consent, as established by Quran and Hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you used something belonging to another Muslim—a book, a tool, or even digital content—without their clear, willing permission. Does this hadith make you feel any unease? What steps can you take today to rectify that or seek forgiveness?
?
When someone accuses you of wrongdoing, do you immediately feel defensive, or do you trust that the burden is on the accuser? How does this prophetic principle change the way you respond to false accusations?
?
Consider a business deal you are part of. Are there any hidden defects or terms you have not disclosed to the other party? The Quran permits trade only by mutual consent—are you truly giving that consent to others, or are they operating on incomplete information?

Enjoyment Without Burden is for Paradise
Al-Nasafi states that enjoyment without burden is for Paradise, citing Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that ultimate effortless enjoyment is a reward for Paradise, not for this world, and that fasting is the means to that reward.

Core Idea
Paradise is the place of effortless enjoyment; this world requires effortful obedience.

This lesson opens with a foundational principle: the full enjoyment of eating and drinking without any burden is only promised in Paradise. Al-Nasafi cites a Quranic verse to show that fasting in this life is what earns that effortless reward.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle and verse
Read the Arabic statement about enjoyment without burden and the Quranic verse that supports it. Notice the connection between 'what you sent ahead' and fasting.
The verse is from Surah al-Haqqah (69:24).
2
Understand the interpretation
Al-Nasafi interprets 'the past days' as days of fasting. Reflect on how acts of worship in this world become the means for reward in the next.
3
Apply the principle
Consider why this principle matters: it reminds believers that worldly pleasures often come with effort, but true rest is in Paradise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن التمتع بلا تحمل عناء التكليف موعود في الجنة كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿كُلُوا وَاشْرَبُوا هَنِيئًا بِمَا أَسْلَفْتُمْ فِي الْأَيَّامِ الْخَالِيَةِ﴾ أي صُمْتُم في الأيام الخالية. وقد أمر الله تعالى عباده بالصوم حيث قال: ﴿فَمَنْ شَهِدَ مِنْكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمْهُ﴾ وقال: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ كَمَا كُتِبَ عَلَى الَّذِينَ مِنْ قَبْلِكُمْ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَتَّقُونَ﴾ فما دام العبد مؤمناً عاقلاً بالغاً لا يسقط عنه الصوم وكذا سائر الفرائض كالصلاة والزكاة بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ وَآتُوا الزَّكَاةَ﴾ بخلاف المريض والمسافر حيث أُبيح لهما الأكل والشرب. والصوم أفضل لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَعِدَّةٌ مِنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَنْ تَصُومُوا خَيْرٌ لَكُمْ إِنْ كُنْتُمْ تَعْلَمُونَ﴾ بخلاف الحائض والنفساء حيث لا تصوم ولا تصلي وتقضي الصوم ولا تقضي الصلاة؛ لأن في قضاء الصلاة حرجًا لتضاعفها ولا حرج في قضاء الصوم.
And because enjoyment without bearing the burden of obligation is promised in Paradise, as Allah Almighty said: 'Eat and drink pleasantly for what you sent ahead in the past days' – meaning you fasted in the past days. And Allah Almighty commanded His servants to fast when He said: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it.' And He said: 'O you who have believed, fasting is prescribed for you as it was prescribed for those before you, that you may become righteous.' So as long as the servant is a believer, sane, and adult, fasting is not lifted from him, and likewise other obligations such as prayer and zakat by His saying: 'Establish prayer and give zakat.' Unlike the sick and the traveler, for whom eating and drinking are permitted. And fasting is better because of His saying: 'Then a number from other days' and His saying: 'And to fast is better for you, if you only knew.' Unlike the menstruating and postpartum woman, who do not fast nor pray, but they make up the fast and do not make up the prayer, because in making up the prayer there is hardship due to its multiplicity, and there is no hardship in making up the fast.

Commentary

Finally, a distinction is made for menstruating and postpartum women: they must make up missed fasts but not missed prayers, because making up prayers would cause excessive hardship due to their frequency.

Fasting and Prayer Remain Obligatory for Believers
Al-Nasafi affirms that fasting and other obligations are binding on believers except for valid excuses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that fasting and other obligations like prayer and zakat remain binding on every believer who is sane and adult, with exceptions for the sick and traveler, and that fasting is superior to not fasting.

Core Idea
Obligations are permanent for believers; exceptions are temporary concessions.

Al-Nasafi now turns to the practical ruling: the commands to fast, pray, and give zakat are not lifted as long as the believer is sane and adult. However, Allah has permitted the sick and traveler to break the fast. This lesson also highlights the virtue of fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commands
Read the verses that command fasting, prayer, and zakat. Notice that these are addressed to all believers.
2
Understand the conditions
Al-Nasafi states that the obligation remains as long as the person is a believer, sane, and adult. Reflect on why these conditions are necessary for accountability.
3
Note the exceptions
The sick and traveler are allowed to eat and drink during Ramadan. Understand that this is a mercy from Allah.
4
Recognize the virtue of fasting
Even though missing fasts can be made up, the verse 'And to fast is better for you' emphasizes that fasting itself is superior.

Ruling for Menstruating and Postpartum Women
Al-Nasafi gives the ruling for menstruating and postpartum women regarding fasting and prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific ruling for menstruating and postpartum women: they do not fast or pray during their condition, but they make up the missed fasts and not the prayers due to hardship.

Core Idea
Menstruating women make up fasts but not prayers to avoid excessive hardship.

This lesson covers the final exception in the passage. Menstruating and postpartum women are excused from both fasting and prayer during their state, but they are required to make up the missed fasts while being excused from making up prayers. Al-Nasafi explains the wisdom behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the ruling
Read the Arabic statement about menstruating and postpartum women. Note that they do not fast nor pray, and they make up the fast but not the prayer.
2
Understand the reason
Al-Nasafi gives the reason: making up prayers would cause hardship because they are numerous (multiple daily prayers), while fasting is only one month a year.
3
Contrast with other exceptions
Compare this to the sick/traveler exception; they may break the fast and make it up, but they still pray normally.
